The Celestial Sphere's Parts Need a Preponderator
Proof that the celestial sphere's parts need a preponderator to determine their location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument for the existence of a preponderator based on the differentiation of parts in the celestial sphere.

Core Idea
Indistinguishable parts cannot be assigned to different locations without a cause.

This lesson presents a classical kalam argument that uses the structure of the celestial sphere to prove the need for an external preponderator. The argument assumes the sphere is a simple, continuous body, yet its identical parts occupy different positions—a difference that demands an explanation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the initial statement: the celestial sphere is a thick body with parts in its depth and surfaces. Note that all parts are similar in essence.
The sphere is considered simple, so parts are identical in every respect except location.
2
Identify the conclusion
The author argues that the specification of some parts to the depth and others to the surface is a preponderance of a possible without a preponderator.
This is the key step: if all parts are identical, any difference in location is arbitrary without a cause.
3
Consider the objection
The objection claims the sphere is one continuous body without actual parts, so the argument fails.
This objection challenges the very existence of distinct parts in the sphere.
4
Study the rebuttal
The author rebuts by referencing the proof of the indivisible atom (jawhar al-fard): anything divisible must have actual parts. Since the sphere is divisible, it has actual parts.
This rebuttal reinforces that the sphere is composed of distinct parts, preserving the original argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : الفلك جسم ثخين، فيفترض في عمقه أجزاء ، وفي كل واحد [ من (۱) ] سطحيه أجزاء أخرى. وجملة تلك الأجزاء متشابهة في تمام الماهية ، بناء على أن الفلك جسم بسيط، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كان اختصاص بعض تلك الأجزاء بالوقوع في العمق ، والبعض في الوقوع في السطح ورجحاناً للممكن ، لا المرجح لا يقال : الفلك جسم واحد متصل وليس فيه أجزاء بالفعل ، فسقط ما ذكرتموه . لأنا نقول : قد بينا بالدلائل القاطعة في مسألة الجوهر الفرد ، أن كل ما يقبل القسمة ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون مركباً من أجزاء ، كل واحد منها يكون ممتازاً عن صاحبه امتيازاً بالفعل، وإذا كان كذلك فقد سقط هذا العذر .
The sixth: The celestial sphere is a thick body, so parts are assumed in its depth, and in each one of its surfaces there are other parts. All those parts are similar in complete essence, based on the fact that the celestial sphere is a simple body. If this is the case, then the specification of some of those parts to occur in the depth and others to occur on the surface would be a preponderance of a possible without a preponderator. It cannot be said: The celestial sphere is one continuous body and has no parts in actuality, so what you have mentioned falls. For we say: We have already shown with conclusive proofs in the issue of the indivisible atom (jawhar al-fard) that everything divisible must necessarily be composed of parts, each of which is actually distinct from its companion. If this is so, then this excuse has fallen.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument for the need of a preponderator. The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) argues that the celestial sphere (فلك) is a thick body; therefore, it has parts in its depth and on its surfaces. Since the sphere is a simple body, all these parts are identical in essence. Yet some parts happen to be in the depth and others on the surface. This differentiation (اختصاص) of identical parts is a case of preponderance (رجحان) of one possible state over another without a preponderator (مرجح), which is impossible without an external cause. The objection that the celestial sphere is continuous and has no actual parts is rebutted by appealing to the proof of the indivisible atom (جوهر فرد): whatever is divisible is necessarily composed of actually distinct parts. Therefore, the objection fails, and the argument stands.

Key Terms
فلك
جوهر فرد
مرجح
رجحان
جزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes the celestial sphere actually has separate parts, but it is just a logical assumption.
Correction: The argument does not assume actual parts; it deduces them from the sphere's divisibility, as proven by the indivisible atom doctrine.

Checkpoint
How does the author rebut the objection that the celestial sphere is one continuous body without actual parts?
A) By stating that the objection contradicts sensory evidence.
B) By citing the proof of the indivisible atom, showing that anything divisible must have actual parts.
C) By arguing that continuity is impossible.
D) By saying the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The differentiation of identical parts in the celestial sphere demonstrates the need for an external preponderator, and the objection of continuity is refuted by the necessary composition of divisible bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that identical parts cannot be assigned to different locations without a preponderator. Where in your own life have you shifted responsibility for the distinctions in your character—why you are kind in some situations but harsh in others—without acknowledging that there must be a preponderator? What would change if you admitted that those differences are not random but point to a cause beyond yourself?
?
Consider that if even a single part of the celestial sphere cannot exist in one place rather than another without a preponderator, then how could you ever be satisfied with explaining your own choices as mere chance? Will you today identify one decision you made as if you were self-sufficient, and instead attribute it to the One who gives existence to all possibilities?
?
The objection that the sphere is continuous and has no parts was refuted by the proof of the atom. What parts of your own soul have you been treating as continuous, avoiding the hard reality that they are distinct and require you to choose? Are you willing to face that you cannot avoid responsibility by pretending your inner divisions are an unbreakable whole?

The Infinite Void Argument and the Defense Against Sophistry
Proof from infinite directions outside the world and defense against the charge of relying on imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh argument for the existence of an infinite void outside the world and the defense against the charge that it relies on imagination.

Core Idea
Denying clear intellectual judgments leads to sophistry.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument that begins with a seemingly simple observation about distinct spatial directions. It then defends the reliability of such intuitive judgments against a skeptical objection, showing that skepticism about foundational intuitions leads to circular reasoning and ultimately to sophistry. Through this, the author establishes the existence of an infinite void beyond the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main premise
The argument starts with an intuitive judgment: reason clearly distinguishes the direction of the north pole from that of the south pole, implying infinite spaces outside the world.
Notice the author uses 'بديهة العقل' (intuitive reason) as the basis.
3
Understand the objection
The objection claims this judgment comes from the estimative faculty (الوهمية), not reason. Find where this is introduced.
The objection is presented as 'لا يقال' (it cannot be said).
4
Follow the rebuttal's logic
The author argues that accepting the objection leads to a vicious circle (دور): if we only know the source of the judgment through reflection, then intuitive truths depend on theoretical ones, but theoretical truths depend on intuitive ones, making knowledge impossible.
This is the core of the defense against sophistry.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The original nature (الفطرة الأصلية) is trustworthy. Since it clearly distinguishes directions, infinite void exists. Then the world's location in that void requires a preponderator.
The final sentence hints at the need for a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : إن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأنه حصل خارج العالم أحياز لا نهاية لها ، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن الجانب الذي يحاذي القطب الشمالي ، مغاير للجانب الذي يحاذي القطب الجنوبي ، وهذا الامتياز في تلك الجوانب أمر يشهد به صريح العقل . لا يقال : الحكم بهذا المعنى هو فطرة القوة [ الوهمية لا فطرة القوة (٢) ( العقلية . لأنا نقول : [ إن (۳) ] هذا الكلام يوجب (٤) القول بالسفسطة . لأنا إذا وجدنا من صريح [ الفطرة (٥) ] حكماً جازماً ، لا شبهة فيه البتة ، وجب علينا أن نحكم بصحته. إذ لو قلنا : إنه حصل عندنا قوة وهمية لا يجوز قبول حكمها ، وقوة عقلية يجب قبول حكمها ، فعلمنا بأن هذا الحكم الجزم . حكم الوهم لا حكم العقل ، لا يكون معلوماً بالبديهة ، بل بالنظر فحينئذ لا يصير حكم العقل مقبولاً ، إلا بعد هذا التمييز الذي لا يعرف إلا بالنظر ، وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه تتوقف صحة البديهات على صحة النظريات ، لكن من المعلوم بالبديهة أن صحة النظريات موقوفة على صحة البديهيات . وحينئذ يتوقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر ، وذلك دور ، وهو باطل . فعلمنا : أن قول القائل : إن هذا الجزم مقتضى الوهم الكاذب ، لا مقتضى العقل الصادق : يوجب السفسطة . وذلك باطل . فثبت : أن كل ما جزمت به الفطرة الأصلية ، كان حقاً وصدقاً . لكن الفطرة الأصلية جزمت بأن الجانب الذي يلي القطب الشمالي ، متميز عن الجانب الذي يلي القطب الجنوبي ، وهذا يوجب القطع بصحة أحياز لا نهاية لها خارج العالم . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : وقوع كرة العالم في بعض جوانب ذلك الخلاء دون البعض ، يكون رجحاناً لا لمرجح .
The seventh: The intuitive judgment of reason rules that outside the world there are infinite spaces, for we know necessarily that the side facing the north pole is different from the side facing the south pole, and this distinction among those sides is something attested to by clear reason. It cannot be said: This judgment is the nature of the estimative faculty (al-wahmiyya) not the nature of the rational faculty. For we say: This statement leads to the assertion of sophistry. For if we find from the clear [nature] a decisive judgment with no doubt whatsoever, we must rule it to be true. If we say: There exists in us an estimative faculty whose judgment is not acceptable, and a rational faculty whose judgment must be accepted, then our knowledge that this decisive judgment is a judgment of the estimative faculty and not of the rational faculty is not known intuitively but by reflection. Then the judgment of reason is not accepted except after this distinction, which is only known by reflection. On this assumption, the validity of intuitive truths depends on the validity of theoretical truths, but it is known intuitively that the validity of theoretical truths depends on the validity of intuitive truths. Then each one depends on the other, which is a vicious circle, and it is invalid. So we know that the statement of one who says: 'This decisive judgment is the requirement of false imagination, not the requirement of truthful reason' leads to sophistry. And that is invalid. So it is established that everything judged by the original (primal) nature is true and correct. But the original nature judged that the side next to the north pole is distinct from the side next to the south pole, and this necessitates the certainty of the existence of infinite spaces outside the world. And when this is established, we say: The location of the sphere of the world in some sides of that void rather than others is a preponderance without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument and defends it against a skeptical objection. The author argues that reason intuitively judges that there are infinite spaces outside the world because the direction of the north pole is distinct from the south. The objection claims this judgment is from the estimative faculty (الوهمية) not reason. The rebuttal shows that this objection leads to sophistry (سفسطة): if we deny obvious judgments, we cannot claim that reason's judgments are reliable without already appealing to theoretical reasoning, creating a circular dependency between intuitive and theoretical truths. Therefore, the original nature (الفطرة الأصلية) is trustworthy. Since it clearly distinguishes directions, the existence of an infinite void outside the world is established. Then the world's location in a particular part of that void, rather than another, is a preponderance without a preponderator, proving the need for a creator.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
فطرة أصلية
وهمية
سفسطة
دور
خلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The judgment about distinct directions is from the estimative faculty (الوهمية) and therefore unreliable.
Correction: Denying such intuitive judgments leads to a vicious circle and sophistry; the original nature (الفطرة الأصلية) is trustworthy, so the judgment is valid.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that denying the intuitive judgment about directions leads to sophistry?
A) Because it implies that all sensory knowledge is false.
B) Because it creates a circular dependency between intuitive and theoretical truths, making knowledge impossible.
C) Because it contradicts the consensus of philosophers.
D) Because it assumes the existence of imaginary faculties.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive judgment that distinguishes spatial directions is a foundational truth; denying it leads to skepticism and sophistry, thereby confirming the existence of an infinite void beyond the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a clear truth about Allah because an intellectual doubt seemed more sophisticated? What would it mean to trust your fitrah instead of letting sophistry shake your certainty? Commit to one truth you will hold onto without overthinking.
?
The argument says that denying intuitive judgments leads to a vicious circle where nothing can be known. In what areas of your daily life do you overcomplicate simple moral or spiritual truths? What step can you take today to act on your fitrah without excessive analysis?
?
The passage defends the reliability of original human nature. Think of a time when you rationalized away a clear sense of right or wrong. What would change if you accepted that your fitrah is generally truthful? Write down one specific commitment to follow your fitrah this week.

The Eighth Argument: The Sperm-Drop and the Need for a Creator
Proof that the formation of different organs from the sperm-drop requires a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the formation of different organs from the sperm-drop, whether it is simple or composite, demonstrates the necessity of a wise preponderator (Creator) beyond mere natural forces.

Core Idea
Blind nature cannot produce diverse forms; a wise designer is necessary.

This lesson presents a powerful metaphysical argument from biological formation. The author considers two possibilities regarding the sperm-drop's nature—simple or composite—and shows that in either case, the resulting diversity of organs cannot be explained by a non-sentient formative power, thus pointing to a deliberate Creator. As you read the passage, notice how the argument eliminates naturalistic explanations and forces the conclusion of a preponderator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two-case structure
The argument is built as a disjunction: either the sperm-drop is a simple body or it is composite. Each branch leads to the conclusion that without a preponderator, the observed diversity is impossible.
Corresponds to the two 'if' statements in the Arabic.
2
Analyze the first case: simple body
If the sperm-drop is simple, a non-sentient formative power acting on it could only produce uniform effects, not distinct organs like the heart and brain. Such diversity would be an arbitrary preponderance without cause.
Focus on the term 'رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن لا لمرجح'.
3
Analyze the second case: composite body
If the sperm-drop is composite, each simple component would produce only a spherical shape (the only effect a blind force can have on simple matter). Since actual organs are non-spherical, this again implies preponderance without a preponderator.
Note the emphasis on 'كرة' (sphere) as the only possible shape from a blind force.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Both cases lead to the same conclusion: the diversity of organic forms cannot arise from a blind nature, therefore a wise preponderator must exist.
This is the main takeaway for the existence of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : النطفة . إما أن تكون جسماً بسيطاً أو لا تكون ، فإن كان الأول فنقول : القوة المصورة عند القوم قوة عديمة الشعور والإدراك ، فتأثيرها في حصول الصورة القلبية وبعض (١) أجزاء تلك النطفة ، وحصول الصورة الدماغية في الجزء الآخر منها ، يكون رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن لا المرجح ، وإن كان الثاني [ وهو (۲) ] إن لا تكون النطفة جسماً بسيطاً ، فنقول : إذا لم يكن بسيطاً كان مركباً ، والمركب مركب عن البسائط ، فكل واحد من تلك البسائط إذا تكون عنه عضو ، وجب أن يكون ذلك العضو كرة ، لأن تأثير القوة الخالية عن الشعور والإدراك في المادة البسيطة ، لا يكون إلا تأثيراً واحداً ، وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، بل حصلت أشكال مختلفة ، ومخالفة لشكل الكرة ، فحينئذ يلزم منه رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر ، لا لمرجح
The eighth: The sperm-drop. Either it is a simple body or it is not. If it is the first, we say: The formative power according to them is a power devoid of sensation and perception. So its effect in producing the form of the heart and some parts of that sperm-drop, and producing the form of the brain in another part of it, would be a preponderance of one of the two possibilities without a preponderator. And if it is the second, i.e., that the sperm-drop is not a simple body, we say: If it is not simple, it is composite, and the composite is composed of simples. So each one of those simples, if an organ is formed from it, must be spherical, because the effect of a power devoid of sensation and perception on simple matter is only a single effect. And since it is not the case, rather different shapes occurred contrary to the spherical shape, then it follows that there is a preponderance of one of the two possibilities over the other without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument, focusing on the sperm-drop (نطفة) as an example of biological formation. The author considers two cases: either the sperm-drop is a simple body or it is composite. In the first case, if it is simple, the formative power (قوة مصورة) is non-sentient and cannot produce different organs (like heart and brain) from the same simple matter; that would be an arbitrary preponderance without a preponderator. In the second case, if it is composite, each simple component would produce only a spherical shape due to the uniformity of the non-sentient power. Since actual organs have diverse shapes (not spherical), this again requires a preponderator. Thus, whether the sperm-drop is simple or composite, the formation of different organs cannot be explained by a blind natural force, proving the need for a wise creator.

Key Terms

قوة مصورة
بسيط
مركب
كرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This is a biological argument that can be refuted by modern embryology.
Correction: The argument is metaphysical, not biological. It uses the observed diversity as a premise to argue that a blind natural force cannot produce such order, regardless of the biological mechanism.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if the sperm-drop were composite and each simple part formed an organ?
A) The organs would all be identical.
B) Each organ would be spherical.
C) The organs would be perfectly formed.
D) The power would be unable to act.

Key Takeaway
The argument from the sperm-drop shows that whether it is simple or composite, the existence of diverse, non-spherical organs cannot be explained by a blind natural force, proving the necessity of a wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own development from a mere drop, do you truly believe that blind chance could have produced the intricate organs you rely on? How does this argument challenge any lingering doubt you have about the necessity of a Creator?
?
The argument shows that blind nature cannot produce diversity. In your daily life, where do you tend to attribute order to mere coincidence rather than to Allah's wisdom? What would it take for you to consciously see His hand in the ordinary?
?
If the formation of your own heart and brain points directly to a wise Creator, what does that demand from you in terms of gratitude and submission? Are you living as if you are a product of random chance, or as a purposeful creation accountable to your Maker?

The Philosophers' Nine Answers to the Objection
Enumerates nine answers from philosophers on creation's delay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' nine answers to the objection about the delay of creation all converge on the idea that either a necessary condition was absent in eternity or that the possible does not require a preponderant.

Core Idea
All nine answers point to one thing: a missing condition or a preponderance without a cause.

Philosophers responded to the objection that God should have created the world earlier by offering nine distinct answers. They claim that all nine boil down to a single insight: either a necessary condition for creation was absent in eternity, or the possible can be preponderant without a preponderant. This passage presents each answer in turn, emphasizing the role of conditions and the possibility of uncaused preponderance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the common thread
Notice how the author begins by stating that all nine answers reduce to one point. Look for the two components: absence of a condition or the possible not needing a preponderant.
3
Examine the first five answers
These answers focus on the absence of a condition (e.g., the specific time, the time containing benefit, etc.). Understand how each answer uses the idea of a missing condition.
4
Examine the last four answers
Answers 6, 8, and 9 deal with preponderance without a preponderant. Answer 7 is about the condition for preceding. Notice how they differ from the first five.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the philosophers would offer these answers. How do they defend the idea that the world's creation at a specific time is rational?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا آخر الكلام في إيراد السؤالات على الدلالة التي لخصناها للفلاسفة والله أعلم وجهين: قالت الفلاسفة: أما الجوابات التسعة التي ذكرتموها، فالكلام عليها من الأول: أن نقول: حاصل هذه الوجوه التسعة، يرجع إلى حرف واحد، وهو أن كل ما لا بد منه في إيجاد العالم ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، أو أن الممكن لا يحتاج إلى المرجح.

أما الجواب الأول: فلأنكم قلتم: إن إرادة الله تعلقت بإيجاد العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين، ثم إن ذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً في الأزل، فالحاصل أن العالم إنما لم يوجد في الأزل، لأن حضور ذلك الوقت شرط حدوث العالم، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً في الأزل، ففات التخليق والإيجاد لفوات شرطه.

وأما الجواب الثاني: فهو أن شرط دخول العالم في حضور ذلك الوقت المشتمل على تلك المصلحة، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً في الأزل، فلم يحصل العالم لفوات شرطه.

وأما الجواب الثالث: فهو أن الوقت الذي علم الله، أن العالم يوجد فيه، شرط لحدوث العالم فيه، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً في الأزل، فالعالم لم يوجد، لأن شرطه ما كان حاضراً.

وأما الجواب الرابع: فهو أن انقضاء الأزل، شرط لحدوث العالم، وذلك الشرط ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لفوات الشرط.

وأما الجواب الخامس: فهو أن كون العالم ممكن الوجود، شرط لصدوره عن المؤثر، وهذا الإمكان ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات حدوث العالم لفوات شرطه.

وأما الجواب السادس: فحاصله يرجع إلى أنه وقع في ذلك الوقت المعين، مع جواز وقوعه في سائر الأوقات، لا لمخصص ولا لمرجح. فههنا التزام أن رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر، وقع لا لمرجح.

وأما الجواب السابع: فحاصله راجع إلى أن جواز التقديم مشروط بحصول الوقت قبل ذلك الوقت ولم يحصل الوقت، فامتنع حصول التقديم، ففات الإحداث قبل ذلك لفوات شرطه.

وأما الجواب الثامن: فحاصله راجع إلى أنه يمكن رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح، وهكذا القول في الجواب التاسع.
And here ends the speech on presenting the objections to the proof that we summarized for the philosophers, and God knows best, two aspects: The philosophers said: As for the nine answers which you mentioned, the discussion about them begins with the first: that we say: The summary of these nine aspects amounts to a single point, namely that everything necessary for the existence of the world was not present in eternity, or that the possible does not need a preponderant. First answer: because you said: God's will was attached to bringing the world into existence at that specific time, then that time was not present in eternity, so the result is that the world was not brought into existence in eternity because the presence of that time is a condition for the occurrence of the world, and that time was not present in eternity, so creation and bringing into existence were missed due to the absence of its condition. Second answer: it is that the condition for the world's entry into the presence of that time containing that benefit, and that time was not present in eternity, so the world did not occur due to the absence of its condition. Third answer: it is that the time which God knew that the world would exist in it is a condition for its occurrence in it, and that time was not present in eternity, so the world did not exist because its condition was not present. Fourth answer: it is that the passing away of eternity is a condition for the occurrence of the world, and that condition was not present in eternity, so bringing into existence was missed due to the absence of the condition. Fifth answer: it is that the world's being possible of existence is a condition for its emanating from the agent, and this possibility was not present in eternity, so the occurrence of the world was missed due to the absence of its condition. Sixth answer: its summary reduces to that it occurred at that specific time, with the permissibility of its occurring at all other times, without a particularizer or preponderant. So here there is an admission that the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other occurred without a preponderant. Seventh answer: its summary goes back to that the permissibility of preceding is conditioned by the occurrence of the time before that time, and the time did not occur, so the occurrence of preceding was prevented, so bringing into existence before that was missed due to the absence of its condition. Eighth answer: its summary goes back to that it is possible for one side of the possible to be preponderant over the other without a preponderant, and similarly for the ninth answer.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' response to an objection concerning why the world was created at a specific time rather than in eternity. The response begins by summarizing that all nine answers revolve around one point: either a necessary condition for the world's existence was absent in eternity, or the possible does not require a preponderant. Then each answer is detailed: (1) the specific time was not present in eternity; (2) the time containing the benefit was absent; (3) the time God knew was absent; (4) the passing of eternity itself is a condition; (5) the possibility of the world was not present in eternity; (6) the occurrence without a preponderant is admitted; (7) the permissibility of being earlier is conditioned on a prior time that did not exist; (8) and (9) essentially repeat that preponderance can occur without a preponderant. Each answer emphasizes that the absence of a condition prevented earlier creation.

Key Terms
إيجاد العالم
الأزل
شرط
مرجح
مخصص
ممكن
رجحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the philosophers are denying causality because they admit preponderance without a preponderant.
Correction: The philosophers are not denying causality; they are arguing that the specific condition for the world's creation was absent in eternity, or that preponderance can occur without an external cause in the case of God's will, which is not a denial of causality but an affirmation of divine freedom.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the common thread among all nine answers?
A) God's will is eternal and unchanging.
B) Either a necessary condition was absent in eternity, or the possible does not need a preponderant.
C) The world is eternal and was never created.
D) The philosophers reject the concept of conditions.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' nine answers all converge on the idea that the world's creation was delayed due to the absence of a condition, or that preponderance can occur without a preponderant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers admit that preponderance can occur without a preponderant. Is there an area of your life where you are demanding to see a clear reason for God's decree, when He simply wills it? Are you willing to surrender to His will without understanding the cause?
?
If the world's creation was tied to a specific time because of a missing condition, what 'condition' might you be neglecting in your own life—a condition that, once met, would unlock a blessing you've been waiting for? Will you seek that condition today?
?
All nine answers reduce to one point: either a condition was absent or no cause is needed. Does the thought that some things in your life have no cause you can discern unsettle you? Can you trust that God's delay is not neglect but wisdom, and that His silence is not rejection but preparation?

Nine Responses and Their Reduction to Two Positions
Enumeration of nine answers and their reduction to two positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nine proposed answers to why the world did not exist in eternity and how they reduce to two fundamental positions.

Core Idea
Nine answers collapse into two: the possible needs no cause, or conditions for causality were absent.

The author presents nine answers that could be given to explain why the world did not exist in eternity despite God's power. These answers introduce various missing conditions. After listing them, the author distills them into two core positions, setting the stage for refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the nine answers
Read the Arabic and translation for each of the nine answers. Notice the pattern: each answer introduces a supposed missing condition.
The first answer is incomplete at the start.
2
Identify the reduction
After the ninth answer, the author states that all nine reduce to two positions. Find that sentence and understand how each answer fits.
3
Understand the two positions
The two positions are: (1) the possible does not need a determinant, and (2) the necessary conditions for causality were not present in eternity. Reflect on why these are the core claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً في الأزل، ففات التخليق والإيجاد لفوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب الثاني: فهو أن شرط دخول العالم في حضور ذلك الوقت المشتمل على تلك المصلحة، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً في الأزل، فلم يحصل العالم لفوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب الثالث: فهو أن الوقت الذي علم الله أن العالم يوجد فيه، شرط لحدوث العالم فيه، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً في الأزل، فالعالم لم يوجد، لأن شرطه ما كان حاضراً.
وأما الجواب الرابع: فهو أن انقضاء الأزل، شرط لحدوث العالم، وذلك الشرط ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لفوات الشرط.
وأما الجواب الخامس: فهو أن كون العالم ممكن الوجود، شرط لصدوره عن المؤثر، وهذا الإمكان ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات حدوث العالم لفوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب السادس: فحاصله يرجع إلى أنه وقع في ذلك الوقت المعين، مع جواز وقوعه في سائر الأوقات، لا لمخصص ولا لمرجح. فههنا التزام أن رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر، وقع لا لمرجح.
وأما الجواب السابع: فحاصله راجع إلى أن جواز التقديم مشروط بحصول الوقت قبل ذلك الوقت، ولم يحصل الوقت، فامتنع حصول التقديم، ففات الإحداث قبل ذلك لفوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب الثامن: فحاصله راجع إلى أنه يمكن رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح، وهكذا القول في الجواب التاسع.
فثبت: أن هذه الجوابات التسعة راجعة، إما إلى القول بأن الممكن المتساوي الطرفين غني عن المرجح والمؤثر، وإما إلى القول بأن كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية، ما كان حاصلا في الأزل. إلا أنا نقول: القول بأن الممكن غني عن المؤثر: قول باطل، من وجهين:
الأول: أنه مقدمة بديهية أولية فالقدح فيه قدح في البديهيات.
والثاني: إنا إذا حكمنا بأن الممكن قد يترجح أحد طرفيه على الآخر لا لمرجح أصلا، فحينئذ لا يمكننا أن نستدل بحدوث المحدثات، وبإمكان الممكنات على افتقارها إلى مرجح ومؤثر، وحينئذ ينسد علينا باب إثبات الصانع.
وأما القول بأن كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، فقد أبطلناه. حيث ذكرنا: أن مجموع ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية، إن لم يكن حاصلا في الأزل، ثم صار حاصلا فيما لا يزال، فحينئذ إن قلنا: إنه حدث لا عن مؤثر، عاد المحال المذكور. وإن قلنا: إنه حدث لمؤثر، عاد التقسيم في كيفية تأثير المؤثر في تحصيل ذلك المجموع. فثبت: أن هذه الأجوبة التسعة ليست أجوبة عن الحجة البتة أصلا.
واعلم أن قولنا: كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية، يدخل فيه ذات المؤثر، والشرائط المعتبرة في مؤثرية المؤثر، وزوال جميع الموانع، وحضور الوقت المناسب، وحضور المصالح المعتبرة. والأجوبة التسعة مشتركة في أن أحد هذه[؟]
The world, and that time was not present in eternity, so creation and bringing into existence were missed due to the absence of its condition.
As for the second answer: it is that the condition of the world's entering into the presence of that time which includes that benefit, and that time was not present in eternity, so the world did not occur due to the absence of its condition.
As for the third answer: it is that the time which God knew that the world would come into existence in it is a condition for the occurrence of the world in it, and that time was not present in eternity, so the world did not exist, because its condition was not present.
As for the fourth answer: it is that the termination of eternity is a condition for the occurrence of the world, and that condition was not attained in eternity, so creation was missed due to the absence of the condition.
As for the fifth answer: it is that the world's being possible of existence is a condition for its emanating from the efficient cause, and this possibility was not attained in eternity, so the occurrence of the world was missed due to the absence of its condition.
As for the sixth answer: its summary returns to the claim that it occurred at that specific time, despite the permissibility of its occurrence at all other times, not due to a specificator nor a determinant. So here there is an admission that the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other occurred without a determinant.
As for the seventh answer: its summary returns to the claim that the permissibility of priority is conditioned upon the occurrence of a time before that time, and the time did not occur, so the occurrence of priority was impossible, so bringing into existence before that was missed due to the absence of its condition.
As for the eighth answer: its summary returns to the claim that it is possible for one side of the possible to preponderate over the other without a determinant, and likewise the statement regarding the ninth answer.
Thus it is established: that these nine answers reduce either to the statement that the possible with equal two sides is independent of the determinant and the efficient cause, or to the statement that everything necessary for efficient causality was not obtained in eternity. However, we say: the statement that the possible is independent of the efficient cause is a false statement, in two respects:
First: it is a primary self-evident premise, so attacking it is an attack on self-evident truths.
Second: if we judge that the possible may have one of its sides preponderate over the other without any determinant at all, then we cannot infer from the occurrence of originated things and the possibility of possible things their need for a determinant and efficient cause, and then the door of proving the Maker is closed to us.
As for the statement that everything necessary for efficient causality was not obtained in eternity, we have already refuted it. Where we mentioned: that the sum of what is necessary for efficient causality, if it was not obtained in eternity, then came to be obtained in what is not yet, then if we say: it occurred without an efficient cause, the aforementioned impossibility returns. And if we say: it occurred by an efficient cause, the division returns concerning the manner of the efficient cause's effect in producing that sum. Thus it is established: that these nine answers are not answers to the argument at all.
And know that our statement: everything necessary for efficient causality includes the essence of the efficient cause, the conditions considered in the efficient causality of the efficient cause, the removal of all impediments, the presence of the appropriate time, and the presence of considered benefits. And the nine answers share in that one of these[?]
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of nine answers that skeptics might give to the argument for the temporal creation of the world. The author first lists the nine answers (though the first is partially given at the start of the page, likely continued from previous pages). Each answer attempts to explain why the world did not exist in eternity despite God's power, by positing missing conditions such as the absence of the appropriate time, the lack of the world's possibility, or the absence of a determinant. After listing them, the author distills the nine answers into two essential positions: either the possible does not require a determinant (meaning it can come into existence without a cause), or the conditions necessary for God's efficient causality were not present in eternity. The author then refutes both positions. The first position (that the possible needs no determinant) is refuted in two ways: (1) it contradicts a self-evident axiom, and (2) it undermines the entire proof for a Creator, because if things can happen without a cause, we cannot infer God from creation. The second position (that necessary conditions were absent in eternity) is refuted by pointing out a dilemma: if the set of conditions came to be later, it either arose without a cause (contradiction) or by a cause (leading to infinite regress). The passage concludes that none of the nine answers are valid. The final sentence begins to clarify what 'everything necessary for efficient causality' includes, but is cut off at the page break.

أزل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think each answer is a separate, independent objection.
Correction: The author shows they all reduce to two core positions, making refutation systematic.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the nine answers reduce to which two positions?
A) That the world is eternal, or that God is not omnipotent.
B) That the possible needs no determinant, or that necessary conditions for causality were absent in eternity.
C) That creation requires a specific time, or that God creates randomly.
D) That the world is impossible, or that God does not create temporally.

Key Takeaway
The nine answers to the timing objection all reduce to two fundamental claims, which the author will refute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful delay in your own life—a prayer unanswered, a goal unachieved—do you tend to assume that God is somehow bound by conditions you cannot see, or that He simply acts without reason? Which of the two root positions does your heart lean toward, and what does that reveal about your understanding of His wisdom? Can you surrender your need to know the 'when' and trust the 'Who' instead?
?
The author reduces nine complex arguments to just two core assumptions. In your own thinking about God's actions, what are the unexamined assumptions you carry? Are you willing to have them reduced and tested against revelation, even if it means letting go of a comfortable explanation?
?
If God's timing of creation was not arbitrary but based on conditions that were absent in eternity, does that change how you view His delays in your life? How can you actively wait—not passively, but with hope—for the missing conditions to be fulfilled, knowing that He is the one who brings them about?

Refutation of the Two Core Positions
Refutation of the two positions derived from the nine answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the claim that the possible needs no determinant and the claim that necessary conditions were absent in eternity.

Core Idea
Both positions are self-defeating: one violates self-evident truths, the other leads to an infinite regress.

After reducing the nine answers to two core positions, the author now refutes them. He shows that the first position (possible independent of cause) contradicts a basic axiom and destroys the proof for a Creator. The second position (conditions absent) leads to a dilemma that either reintroduces the absurdity or requires an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the first position
Read the passage from 'إلا أنا نقول' to 'ينسد علينا باب إثبات الصانع'. Notice the two arguments against the first position.
The first refutation is based on self-evidence, the second on practical consequence.
2
Read the refutation of the second position
Read the next part where the author refutes the claim that conditions were absent. Note the dilemma: either the conditions arose without a cause (impossible) or by a cause (regress).
3
Understand the final conclusion
The author concludes that none of the nine answers are valid. Also note the final sentence listing what 'necessary for efficient causality' includes.
The text cuts off but the key points are clear.

العالم، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً في الأزل، ففات التخليق والإيجاد لفوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب الثاني: فهو أن شرط دخول العالم في حضور ذلك الوقت المشتمل على تلك المصلحة، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً في الأزل، فلم يحصل العالم لفوات شرطه.
وأما الجواب الثالث: فهو أن الوقت الذي علم الله أن العالم يوجد فيه، شرط لحدوث العالم فيه، وذلك الوقت ما كان حاضراً في الأزل، فالعالم لم يوجد، لأن شرطه ما كان حاضراً.
وأما الجواب الرابع: فهو أن انقضاء الأزل، شرط لحدوث العالم، وذلك الشرط ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات الإيجاد لفوات الشرط.
وأما الجواب الخامس: فهو أن كون العالم ممكن الوجود، شرط لصدوره عن المؤثر، وهذا الإمكان ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، ففات حدوث العالم لفوات شرطه.

First Argument Against the Second Answer: Eternal Beneficial Time
Author presents a dilemma about the beneficial time's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that arises if one claims God acted at a specific time due to a beneficial time, and how it leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
If the beneficial time was eternal, creation must have been eternal; if not, God lacked something necessary.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful argument against the second answer to the problem of divine action. The opponent says God acted at a particular time because that time had a preponderant benefit. The author forces a dilemma: either that beneficial time existed from eternity or it did not. Both options lead to unacceptable consequences, as we will see.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the two possibilities presented.
The passage is a concise logical argument; pay attention to each conditional.
2
Identify the dilemma
Notice the two horns: if the beneficial time was eternal, then the act should be eternal; if not, then something necessary for God's causality was absent in eternity.
The author uses a hypothetical syllogism.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author says the second option is already proven false, so the first must also be false, refuting the whole answer.
Consider why the second horn is considered false by the author's earlier proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن الوقت المشتمل على المصلحة الراجحة . هل كان حاصلا في الأزل ، أو ما كان حاصلا . فإن كان حاصلا ، لزم حصول الفعل في الأزل . وإن قلنا : إنه لو كان حاصلا في الأزل ، مع أن ذلك الوقت أحد الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية ، فحينئذ يكون هذا قولاً بأن كل ما لا بد منه في كونه تعالى مؤثراً في الفعل ، وموجداً له : ما كان حاصلا في الأزل . لكنا بينا بالدليل أن ذلك باطل.
The first proof: The time that contains the preponderant benefit. Was it present in eternity, or was it not present? If it was present, then the occurrence of the act in eternity would be necessary. And if we say that if it was present in eternity, while that time is one of the matters considered in effective influence, then this would be a saying that everything necessary for Him, exalted, to be effective in the act and to bring it into existence was not present in eternity. But we have shown by proof that this is false.
Source pages p. 66

Key Terms
الوقت (al-waqt)
المصلحة الراجحة (al-maṣlaḥa al-rājiḥa)
الأزل (al-azal)
المؤثرية (al-muʼaththariyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The beneficial time is a condition for God's action that does not affect His eternity.
Correction: The argument shows that if the beneficial time is considered necessary for God's effectiveness, then its absence in eternity would imply God was not fully effective, contradicting His perfection.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility considered in the argument?
A) The beneficial time was not present in eternity
B) The beneficial time was present in eternity
C) The beneficial time was created
D) The beneficial time is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The assumption of a beneficial time as a condition for divine action forces an unacceptable choice between eternal creation and a deficiency in God's causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The dilemma shows that any external condition necessary for God's action leads to either eternal creation or a deficiency in God. What 'conditions' (e.g., personal purity, community support, favorable events) do you unconsciously treat as prerequisites for God to work in your life? Will you renounce that mindset and trust His perfect will now?
?

Fifth Argument: Tiny Changes Don't Affect Interests
Refutes the answer by arguing that even if God considers interests, a minute time change does not affect them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical force of the fifth argument: that even if God considers interests, minuscule changes in time or magnitude would not alter any interest, thus disproving the specific time's necessity.

Core Idea
Tiny changes don't affect interests, so the specific time is not required for benefits.

This lesson presents the fifth argument against the claim that God's choice of creation time was dictated by interest. The argument concedes that God may consider interests, but then shows that even infinitesimal changes would leave interests unaffected. Therefore, the specific timing cannot be necessary for any interest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a concession followed by examples.
The passage begins with 'هب أنه تعالى يراعي المصالح' (Suppose that He considers interests).
2
Understand the hypothetical concession
The argument grants the opponent's premise that God does consider interests. This is for the sake of argument.
3
Grasp the two examples
The first example is a tiny change in creation time (part of a glance). The second is a tiny change in the size of the celestial sphere (a single atom).
Both examples emphasize minuteness: 'جزء من ألف جزء من لمحة' and 'جوهراً فرداً'.
4
See the invariability of interests
The argument states that neither change would alter any interest of the obligated beings, especially if God does not reveal the change.
5
Conclude the invalidity of the opponent's claim
Since the specific time could be changed without affecting interests, the opponent's claim that it was chosen for a specific benefit is baseless.
The passage ends with 'فيثبت: أن هذا الذي قالوه فاسد' (Thus it is established that what they said is invalid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة في إبطاله : أن نقول : هب أنه تعالى يراعي المصالح ، إلا أن من المعلوم بالضرورة أنه تعالى لو قدم خلق العالم على الوقت الذي خلقه فيه بجزء من ألف جزء من لمحة واحدة ، أو أخر خلقه عن ذلك الوقت بهذا القدر من الوقت ، فإنه لا يتفاوت شيء من مصالح المكلفين البتة ، لا سيما إذا قلنا : إن الله تعالى لا يطلعهم على [ هذا ] القدر من التفاوت ، ولا يوقفهم عليه . ولو أنه زاد في مقدار الفلك [ الأعظم ] جوهراً فرداً ، أو نقص هذا المقدار عنه . فإنه لا يتفاوت شيء من مصالح المكلفين البتة . فيثبت : أن هذا الذي قالوه فاسد
The fifth argument in refuting it: that we say: suppose that He, exalted, does consider interests, however it is known by necessity that if He advanced the creation of the world before the time in which He created it by a part of a thousand parts of a single glance, or delayed its creation after that time by this amount of time, then none of the interests of the obligated beings would differ at all, especially if we say that God, exalted, does not inform them of this amount of difference, nor does He make them aware of it. And if He increased the magnitude of the [greatest] sphere by a single atom, or decreased this magnitude from it, then none of the interests of the obligated beings would differ at all. Thus it is established that what they said is invalid.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument against the answer. Even granting that God considers the interests of His creatures, it is self-evident that a minuscule change in the time of creation—by a fraction of a moment—would not alter any interest. This is especially true if God does not reveal such minor differences to people. Similarly, a tiny increase or decrease in the size of the celestial sphere would not affect interests. Therefore, the claim that the specific time was chosen for a specific benefit is baseless.

Key Terms
بالضرورة (by necessity)
مصالح المكلفين (interests of the obligated)
التفاوت (difference)
جوهراً فرداً (a single atom)
الفلك الأعظم (the greatest sphere)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might argue that even a small change could affect some interests, but the author insists it is negligible and unknown to creatures.
Correction: The argument is not merely that the change is small, but that it is so minute that no interest would be different at all, especially because God would not even inform creatures of the change.

Checkpoint

A) A comparison to the size of the cosmos.
B) The fact that God is all-powerful.
C) The invariability of interests under minute time changes.
D) The impossibility of changing the past.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument shows that even if God considers interests, infinitesimal changes in time or magnitude do not affect any interest, thereby refuting the claim that a specific time was chosen for a specific benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this argument challenge your impulse to question His wisdom in that moment? Will you consciously repent from that doubt today?
?

What would change if you truly accepted that His timing, even if seemingly arbitrary, does not diminish your real welfare? Refutation of the Seventh Answer: Three Logical Proofs
Razi refutes the seventh objection using three logical proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses three distinct logical arguments to refute the claim that the concept of 'before' is meaningless for the first event.

Core Idea
The concept of 'before' is meaningful even for the first event, proven by necessity, divine priority, and hypothetical possibilities.

Razi now addresses a subtle objection: if there is no time 'before' the first event, how can we ask why it happened then and not earlier? The opponents claim the question is invalid. Razi calls their answer weak and provides three proofs to show that 'beforeness' is a meaningful concept, whether based on necessary intuition, the nature of the Creator, or hypothetical scenarios. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the translation. Notice that Razi explicitly numbers three proofs (الوجه الأول, الثاني, الثالث). Try to get a sense of each before diving in.
2
Focus on the first proof: necessary intuition
The first proof argues that we know by necessity that every originated thing is preceded by its non-existence. If there were no 'beforeness' at all, this innate knowledge would be false. Therefore, 'beforeness' must exist even for the first event.
This proof relies on الفطرة (innate disposition).
3
Analyze the second proof: divine priority
The second proof considers the deity's relationship to time. If God coexists with the world, that implies either the world is eternal (شرك) or God is originated (both impossible). So God must be prior. But His priority cannot be finite, else He would be originated. Thus, His priority implies an infinite hypothetical sequence of events, giving meaning to 'before'.
This is a complex logical branching argument.
4
Examine the third proof: the revolving sphere
Razi asks us to consider the sphere of fixed stars completing exactly one million revolutions from the world's beginning. If this is impossible, then the world's initial state would be impossible, which is absurd. If possible, then 'beforeness' and 'afterness' refer to the counting of such hypothetical events, even if no actual time existed.
This proof uses a thought experiment to show that 'before' can be meaningful without a prior time.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
All three proofs together demonstrate that the seventh answer is weak. The question about 'why this time and not before' is valid because 'before' can refer to prior non-existence, divine priority with infinite potential events, or hypothetical counting.
The key is that 'beforeness' does not require actual prior time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب السابع: وهو قولهم: إن قول القائل: لم حدث العالم في هذا الوقت، ولم يحدث قبله؟ إنما يصح لو حصل قبل هذا الوقت وقت آخر، وعندنا: أن هذا الوقت هو أول الأوقات، فلم يحصل قبله وقت آخر. فسقط هذا السؤال. فنقول: هذا الجواب أيضاً ضعيف. ويدل عليه وجوه:
الأول: إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن عدم كل حادث سابق على وجوده، فلولا حصول القبلية قبل ما فرضتموه أول الحوادث وإلا لما شهدت الفطرة بأن عدم كل شيء قبل وجوده
والثاني: إن إله العالم إما أن يكون مقارناً لوجود العالم، أو يكون سابقاً عليه. والأول يوجب إما قدم العالم أو حدوث الإله، وكلاهما محالان. والثاني على قسمين. لأنه تعالى إما أن يكون سابقاً على العالم بما لو فرضنا الحوادث موجودة، لكانت إما متناهية أو غير متناهية، والأول يوجب حدوث الإله، فبقي الثاني وهو أن الإله متقدم على العالم، بما لو فرضنا الحوادث موجودة، لكانت تلك الحوادث غير متناهية، وحينئذ نقول: إنا مرادنا من القبلية هذا المعنى
والوجه الثالث في إيطال هذا الجواب: إنا نفرض أن فلك الثوابت دار من أول حدوث العالم إلى يومنا هذا، ألف ألف دورة فنقول: هل كان يمكن أن يحدث فلك الثوابت بحيث كان يحصل من أول حدوثه إلى الآن ألف ألف دورة، أم لا؟ فإن كان ذلك ممتنعاً، فقد عاد الحديث المذكور من أن العالم كان ممتنع الوجود لعينه، ثم انقلب ممكن لعينه، وهو محال، وإن كان ذلك ممكناً، فهذا هو المراد من القبلية والبعدية، سواء عبر عنه بهذا اللفظ أم لا
As for the seventh answer: it is their saying: 'The statement of the one who says: Why did the world occur at this time and not occur before it? It is only valid if there was another time before this time, and according to us: this time is the first of times, so no other time occurred before it. Thus this question falls.' We say: This answer is also weak. Evidence for it are proofs:
First: We know by necessity that the non-existence of every temporally originated thing precedes its existence. So if there were no 'beforeness' before what you have assumed to be the first of originated things, then innate disposition would not testify that the non-existence of everything is before its existence.
Second: The deity of the world is either simultaneous with the existence of the world, or prior to it. The first entails either the eternity of the world or the temporal origination of the deity, both of which are impossible. The second has two divisions: because He, exalted, is either prior to the world in a way that if we supposed temporally originated things existed, they would be either finite or infinite. The first entails the temporal origination of the deity, so the second remains: that the deity is prior to the world in a way that if we supposed temporally originated things existed, those temporally originated things would be infinite. Then we say: Our intention from 'beforeness' is this meaning.
The third proof in invalidating this answer: We suppose that the sphere of the fixed stars revolved from the beginning of the world's occurrence until our present day one million revolutions. Then we say: Was it possible for the sphere of the fixed stars to occur such that from its first occurrence until now one million revolutions would take place, or not? If that is impossible, then the aforementioned discussion returns that the world was impossible in its essence, then became possible in its essence, which is absurd. And if that is possible, then this is the intended meaning of beforeness and afterness, whether it is expressed by this term or not.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage refutes the seventh objection (الجواب السابع) of opponents who claim that the question 'Why did the world occur at this time and not before?' is invalid because there is no 'before' the first moment. The author (Razi) declares this answer weak and provides three proofs.

Proof one: We know by necessary intuition that non-existence precedes existence for every originated thing. If there were no 'beforeness' before the first event, this intuition would be false. Therefore, a meaningful 'before' must exist even for the first event, namely the prior non-existence.

Proof two: Consider the deity: either coexistent with the world (which implies either the world is eternal or God is originated—both impossible) or prior to the world. Prior means that if we suppose temporal things existed, they would be infinite (since God's priority cannot be finite without entailing His origination). Thus, the intended 'beforeness' is that God is prior in a way that hypothetical infinite events would precede.

Proof three: Suppose the sphere of fixed stars has revolved one million times from the world's beginning to now. Could it have been created to achieve exactly that many revolutions? If impossible, then the world's existence would have been originally impossible and then became possible, which is absurd. If possible, then 'before' and 'after' refer to this kind of hypothetical counting, not necessarily literal time.

Thus, the three proofs establish that the concept of 'before' is meaningful for the first event, refuting the seventh answer.

Key Terms

البعدية
الحدوث
القدم
الفطرة
الممكن
الممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If there is no time before the first event, the concept of 'before' is meaningless, so the question 'why then and not earlier' is invalid.
Correction: Razi shows that 'before' can refer to prior non-existence, divine priority with infinite hypothetical events, or counting hypothetical possibilities, all of which are meaningful even without actual prior time.

Checkpoint
In the third proof, what does Razi use to illustrate the meaningfulness of 'beforeness'?
A) The existence of a prior time
B) The possibility of a sphere completing a specific number of revolutions from its beginning
C) The necessity of the world's existence
D) The impossibility of divine priority

Key Takeaway
The seventh answer fails because 'beforeness' is meaningful in terms of prior non-existence, divine priority with infinite hypothetical events, and hypothetical counting, all of which refute the claim that no 'before' exists for the first event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate your own existence, do you truly feel the weight of the fact that your non-existence preceded your existence? How does this realization shape your gratitude and humility before Allah?
?
The third proof uses a hypothetical scenario about the revolutions of the fixed stars. Have you ever pondered the precise and intentional nature of Allah's creation in such abstract possibilities? How can this deepen your tawakkul in His wisdom?
?
Razi shows that 'beforeness' is meaningful even without actual time. Have you ever struggled to articulate something about Allah's eternal existence? How does this lesson strengthen your certainty that He is First without needing temporal precedence?

Refuting the Eighth Answer: Preference Requires a Reason
Razi refutes the eighth answer on capability and preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that a capable agent can prefer an action without a separate preferrer is logically untenable.

Core Idea
Preference requires a reason; mere ability cannot tip the scales.

This passage tackles a key argument about divine power and preference. Opponents claimed that a capable being can simply choose without any additional cause. Razi dismantles this by analyzing the very concept of capability and showing that it leads to absurdity or requires an extra factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of Razi's argument. Notice how he first states the eighth answer and then systematically refutes it.
Pay attention to the logical structure: definition, dilemma, experiential evidence, and conceptual analysis.
2
Understand the definition of 'capable'
Razi defines 'capable' (al-qādir) as having equal ability to perform or refrain. This equal relation to both sides is crucial for the dilemma that follows.
If capability were not equal, it would not be capability in the full sense.
3
Analyze the two-horned dilemma
Either this equal capability is sufficient for the specific act (leading to preference without a preferrer, which is absurd) or it is not (requiring an additional factor, collapsing the answer). Identify both horns and their consequences.
The experiential argument and the chance argument reinforce the second horn.
4
Grasp the conceptual point about 'prefers'
Razi asks whether 'prefers' adds a concept beyond being capable. If it does, then preference is not from mere ability but from an additional attribute, further undermining the original claim.
This shows that the opponents' own language implies a separate cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب الثامن: وهو قولهم: القادر يمكنه ترجيح أحد الطرفين على الآخر لا المرجح. فنقول: هذا الجواب ضعيف وذلك لأن القادر هو الذي يكون متمكناً من الفعل، ومن تركه بدلاً عنه. فنسبة هذه القادرية إلى الطرفين على السوية، فإما أن يكون هذا القدر كافياً في أن يكون مصدراً للفعل بعينه، وإما أن لا يكون كافياً فيه. فإن كان كافياً فيه كان ذلك ترجيحاً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا لمرجح وهو محال. وأيضاً: فنحن نجد من أنفسنا وجداناً ضرورياً أن هذا القدر لا يكفي في وقوع أحد الطرفين، بل ما لم أحد الطرفين على الآخر بسبب قصد وميل وترجيح، لم يحصل الرجحان. وأيضاً: فعلى هذا التقدير يكون وقوع هذا الفعل: محض الاتفاق العاري عن كل الأسباب. وأما إن لم يكن مجرد القادرية كافياً في حصول هذا الرجحان، فحينئذ يجب انضمام أمر آخر إليه، وحينئذ يسقط هذا الجواب بالكلية.
واعلم أن قول القائل: القادر يرجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر فهل لقولنا: يرجح مفهوم زائد على كونه قادراً، أو ليس له مفهوم زائد؟ فإن كان الأول، فحينئذ ظهر أن رجحان الفعل على الترك: ما حصل لمجرد كونه قادراً، وإنما حصل عند انضمام هذا المفهوم الزائد إلى أصل القادرية.
As for the eighth answer: it is their saying: 'The capable (al-qādir) can prefer one of the two sides over the other without a preferrer.' We say: This answer is weak. That is because the capable is the one who is able to perform the act and to refrain from it instead. So the relation of this capability to the two sides is equal. Either this capability is sufficient for it to be the source of the specific act, or it is not sufficient for it. If it is sufficient for it, then that would be a preference (tarjīḥ) of one side of the possible over the other without a preferrer, which is absurd. Also: we find from ourselves by necessary intuition that this capability is not sufficient for the occurrence of either side; rather, as long as one does not prefer one side over the other due to intention, inclination, and preference, the preponderance does not occur. Also: on this supposition, the occurrence of this act would be pure coincidence devoid of all causes. And if mere capability is not sufficient for the occurrence of this preponderance, then it is necessary for another thing to be combined with it, and then this answer falls completely.
And know that the saying of the one who says: 'The capable prefers one of his two objects of power over the other' — does our saying 'prefers' have an additional concept beyond his being capable, or does it not have an additional concept? If the first, then it becomes clear that the preponderance of the act over abstention did not occur merely because of his being capable, but rather occurred when this additional concept was combined with the original capability.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage refutes the eighth answer (الجواب الثامن) of opponents who claim that a capable agent can prefer one of two possible actions without a separate preferrer. The author argues this is weak.

First, he defines 'capable' (القادر) as one who has equal ability to perform or refrain. This ability (القادرية) is equally related to both options. If this ability alone suffices to produce the specific act, then that would be a preference without a preferrer, which is absurd—since preference implies a factor that tips the scales. If ability is not sufficient, then we need an additional factor (intention, inclination) to bring about the act; hence the answer collapses.

Second, we have direct inner experience (وجدان) that mere ability does not produce an act; we require a specific intention and preference for one side.

Third, without such preference, the act would be pure chance (اتفاق), which is untenable.

Then the author analyzes the word 'prefers' (يرجح): does it add a concept beyond 'able'? If yes, then preference is not from mere ability but from an additional attribute. This further undermines the original claim.

Thus, the eighth answer is refuted by showing that preference requires a separate cause.

Key Terms
القادر
القادرية
الترجيح
المرجح
الوجدان
الاتفاق
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A capable agent can choose one action over another without any reason or additional cause.
Correction: Choice requires a reason; even the capability itself is equally related to both options, so a separate factor (intention, will) is necessary to tip the balance.

Checkpoint

A) The act would occur without any cause
B) That would entail a preference without a preferrer, which is absurd
C) The agent would be forced to act
D) The act would be impossible

Key Takeaway
Mere capability does not explain why one specific action occurs; a separate preference or cause is necessary.

A Universal Rational Argument to Refute All Objections
A summary universal rational argument to refute all objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single logical dilemma can refute all objections against the principle of preponderance in arguments for God's existence.

Core Idea
A single premise can decide the entire debate: accept it and objections fall, reject it and you lose the proof.

After reviewing the previous answers, the author now presents a sweeping rational argument that cuts through all the detailed objections. Instead of tackling each objection individually, he points to the foundational premise that underlies them all. This lesson introduces that decisive dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author has concluded the previous answers and now begins a new section: answering objections. He offers a universal argument before detailed responses. Read the opening lines to see this transition.
Notice the phrase 'universal rational speech' (kalam kulli 'aqli).
2
Identify the key premise
All objections are aimed at the premise: 'The contingent does not have one of its two sides preponderant over the other except by a preponderant.' Locate this premise in the passage and understand it as the foundation.
This is the principle of determination (tarjeeh).
3
Analyze the dilemma
The author poses a question: Do you accept this premise or deny it? Trace the two branches of the dilemma and their consequences.
The opponent is forced to choose.
4
Consequences of accepting
If the opponent accepts the premise, then all objections fall because they are based on attacking this premise. The author says they are 'corrupt, repelled, and false.'
5
Consequences of denying
If the opponent denies the premise, then they cannot prove the existence of a Necessary Being from the contingency and temporality of the world. This undermines the entire cosmological argument.
The author calls this 'utmost corruption and falsehood.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا آخر الكلام على الأجوبة المذكورة والله أعلم. نشرع الآن في الجواب عن المعارضات المذكورة . واعلم أنا قبل الخوض في الجوابات المفصلة نذكر كلاماً كلياً عقلياً في دفعها : فنقول : إن النقوض المذكورة ، إنما أوردتموها على قولنا : الممكن لا أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجح . فهل أنتم تعترفون بصحة هذه المقدمة أو تنكرونها ؟ فإن سلمتم صحتها فقد زالت تلك النقوض وتلك المعارضات ، لأنها بأسرها واردة على هذه المقدمة . فلما كانت هذه المقدمة صحيحة كانت تلك النقوض والمعارضات كلها فاسدة مدفوعة باطلة . وأما إن منعتم صحة هذه المقدمة ، فحينئذ لا يمكنكم أن تستدلوا بإمكان الممكنات، وحدوث المحدثات، على وجود موجود واجب الوجود . وذلك في نهاية الفساد والبطلان . فهذا كلام كلي في دفع هذه النقوض والمعارضات على سبيل الإجمال .
And here ends the discussion of the mentioned answers, and God knows best. We now begin the answer to the mentioned objections. Know that before we delve into the detailed answers, we present a universal rational speech (kalam kulli 'aqli) in repelling them. So we say: The mentioned criticisms have only been brought against our statement: 'The contingent (al-mumkin) does not have one of its two sides preponderant over the other except by a preponderant (murajjih).' Do you acknowledge the soundness of this premise or deny it? If you concede its soundness, then those criticisms and objections have fallen, because they all bear upon this premise. Since this premise is sound, those criticisms and objections are all corrupt, repelled, and false. But if you deny the soundness of this premise, then you cannot infer from the possibility of contingent things and the temporality of temporal things the existence of a necessarily existent being. And that is the utmost corruption and falsehood. So this is a universal speech in repelling these criticisms and objections in summary fashion.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage serves as a transition and a summary universal refutation. The author concludes the previous answers and announces a new section: the answer to the objections. He proposes a general rational argument before going into details. The key premise is the principle of determination: a contingent being does not become preponderant in one of its two possible states without a preponderant (murajjih). All objections are targeted at this premise. The author poses a dilemma: either the opponent accepts the premise or denies it. If accepted, the objections fall because they rely on attacking the premise, which is now conceded as sound. If denied, the opponent loses the ability to argue for the existence of a necessary being from the contingency and temporality of the world — the very foundation of the cosmological argument. This is presented as a decisive refutation in summary form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is circular because it assumes what it tries to prove.
Correction: It is a dialectical refutation that shows the opponent's inconsistency: if they accept the premise, their objections are self-defeating; if they deny it, they forfeit their own argument for God's existence.

Key Takeaway

Second Objection and the First Maqam: Distinguishing Temporal Origination from Celestial Motion
The author responds to the second objection by citing the proof of the world's temporal origination and distinguishing it from the case of the celestial body's motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the specification of the celestial body's motion does not require a preponderator, unlike the world's origination.

Core Idea
Not every specification needs a cause; only changes from one possibility to another do.

This passage tackles a subtle but critical objection to the cosmological argument for God's existence. The opponent tries to apply the same reasoning used to prove the world's temporal origination to the specific motion of a celestial body—but the author shows a crucial difference. We'll unpack his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The opponent asks: why did the sphere's motion occur in this specific manner? Read the opening lines.
The objection assumes the sphere could have moved differently.
2
Understand the proof of temporal origination
The author reviews the proof that the world's origination at a specific time, while equally possible at all times, requires a preponderator.
This sets up the parallel the opponent tries to draw.
3
See how the opponent applies the proof
The opponent argues that if the sphere can accept contrary motions, its specific motion would also need a preponderator.
This is the challenge the author must answer.
4
Learn the author's key distinction
The author argues that it is not proven that the sphere can accept opposite motions; in fact, it may only be capable of one motion.
This distinguishes the case from the world's origination.
5
Recognize the fallacy
The opponent wrongly assumes that because something is possible in one case, it must be possible in another. The author shows why this does not follow.
Essences do not entail all properties uniformly.

Source Text
